1439152780|4.0|Reading the other reviews of this book, a rather startling conclusion came to me:  I seem to be the only person of the male gender at least to have reviewed this book. How very strange!  I don't think I've run across this phenomenon in all my years of reviewing.  Surely there are males out there who love Jane Eyre as much as I do?  I sincerely don't know.  But the reason I ask the question is that I would venture to surmise that if you loved or even liked Jane Eyre, you will love this book.<br /><br />It's also curious that the women who didn't like this book disliked it for all the reasons I liked it.  The book is dense, the characters intensely literary, the writing full of lovely atmospherics.  So much so that I will also venture to add that anyone who is on a familiar footing with the literary life, for whom the literary life, as Proust puts it, is the only life, will love at least the first part of the book.  Almost every single major character in the book, including the males, is obsessed by books and literature. The first third of the book was truly spellbinding for me, whilst the rest of the book, the unravelling of the many mysteries surrounding Milderhurst Castle and the three weird sisters who inhabit it, becomes rather pat. (N.B.--\\"The the weird sisters\\" here is a whimsical reference to Shakespeare's Macbeth.  I don't usually feel I have to explain my allusions, but some of the reviews here have put me on notice.)<br /><br />Here is what it seems to me that Ms. Morton was striving to write or conjure in the first part of the book: Mystery, yes, but not the type of mystery that ever gets unwound or explained, the mystery of time and human personality as it flickers through life. She tries herein to combine that deeper mystery with her vintage standard mystery novel, and the result is unfortunately but inevitably rather strained.  There are several passages, as well as the title of the book itself, which capture this deeper mystery quite well.  My personal favourite is when our intrepid heroine is looking through old black-and-white photographs:  \\"There's something deeply nostalgic about black-and-white photographs, the absence of colour a visual rendering of the deepening funnel of time.\\" As another reviewer has put it: \\"Wow!\\"<br /><br />The plot, as such, has been rehashed by the other reviewers, but plot is not what entranced me about this novel.  It's the deeper mysteries of the human soul caught in time that truly captivate, the oddness of time itself, the literary person as outcast, who has his/her own Milderhurst Castle within her/him, with its walls, from which there is ultimately no escape, constantly haunting the soul with half-remembered voices within them, with Keats's unheard music, with imaginings of unsounded depths.en our intrepid heroine is looking through old black-and-white photographs:  \\"There's something deeply nostalgic about black-and-white photographs, the absence of colour a visual rendering of the deepening funnel of time.\\" As another reviewer has put it: \\"Wow!\\"    The plot, as such, has been rehashed by the other reviewers, but plot is not what entranced me about this novel. The Bourne Identity is a gripping tale of a man, his origins, his uncertain present and conspiracies that entangle a range of ruthless or unsuspecting people somehow associated with the man they dont really know. The movie version is set in a modern setting and far different from this novel but both are definitely greatly enjoyable taken individually. The book set quite a few decades ago (when it was written) deals with the enigma known globally as Carlos the jackal. A race against time across varying locations in differing situations that make you glued to the book you hold closer and closer as plans unfold, dead bodies discovered, news and its targets manipulated and an ever changing landscape for the protagonist with an unknown past and bleak future. The man is plagued by these things, not knowing where it comes from and what it means. The novel is an exploration of not just the man's mind but the unexpected possibilities of covert missions, deception and the dangerous world of terrorism. This book does work as a standalone but of course Ludlum wrote 2 more of Bourne adventures. The thrill of the first ignites a passion to read on. Pick this up and forget the movie version for the time being. Both are great in their uniqueness. There are no cell phones or other gadgetry in this old novel but the novel isn't dated. If anything it proves how effective a trained person can be when dealt with uncertain and life-threatening circumstances. A real masterpiece from Ludlum.

0756404738|3.0|First, I listened to this as an MP3 and I think Nick Podehl's narration is outstanding.    The same cannot be said, alas, for Rothfuss' \\"second day\\" of the already monolithic \\"Kingkiller Chronicles.\\"    Note: Patrick Rothfuss is a real writer, better than I could ever hope to be. So I write as a reader.    Everybody talks about the problems of a second book in a trilogy: you're past the introduction of plot lines and lead characters, need to move them on, but inevitably, the end is not satisfying (since it's not really the end).     Rothfuss took many more years than some fans wished to write this second installment. The guess here is that it needed a *lot* of editing. Truth is, it could have taken a lot more. This book is 40 percent too long for its own good.    Oh, I enjoyed it - sort of. He's a fine writer (with some exceptions; see below) and he did manage to string me along. That's the way I felt about \\"Name of the Wind,\\" too: it was a bit of a chore to finish, but I did, and that is to his credit as a writer.    Some issues here:    1) The conceit of telling the story in three \\"days\\" just doesn't wash. This book took 40+ hours to listen to; unless the days in Kvothe's world are twice as long as ours, and they all stayed up all night, this didn't take one \\"day.\\" (And remember, this is verbatim what he's telling Chronicler, so there is no excuse there.) Had Rothfuss (and his editors) recognized this disconnect, perhaps it would have inspired some much needed trimming.    2) Rothfuss is a fine writer. But at this ungodly length, it seems even he got lazy, or more likely, tired. Usually good at turning a simile (though far too often here he uses three when one would have done), by the last hours (i.e. pages) of this novel he was resorting to cliche after cliche - \\"wet as a drowned rat,\\" \\"knocked me over with a feather,\\" etc.     3) Melodrama. Far, far too often, Kvothe's various foils (Albaran, Denna, Bast, Vashet, etc.) seesaw wildly in their emotions, one momentseething with fury and freaking out, literally the next turning meek with forgiveness or a wee smile. A couple times you might buy, but Rothfuss does this so often that it strains credulity and makes the reader painfully aware that's it's just cheap melodrama. Manipulative and false, it makes these people seem like, well, jerks. Or crazy. Or bipolar. Or borderline. Or something.    4) Like other reviewers, I see this book as more a collection of long tales - \\"The Further Adventures of Kvothe the Kingkiller\\" - than a novel. Rothfuss is meandering, plain and simple. The tales are interesting in their own right - Felurian, the Adem, Alveron, the Cthaeh, the bandits and Cinder (it is fascinating to hear these names pronounced and only later see them spelled out) - but they honestly don't do much to push the main story forward. (Or perhaps they do; perhaps Rothfuss really is a genius and will make all these comments moot with a spectacular finish.)I will say that the book's final twist was a nifty one; perhaps not totally unexpected, but nifty even so.    I liked the book enough to listen to it for more than 40 hours. But honestly, as I approached the end I had some of the same feeling I get when running a marathon: I couldn't wait to cross the finish line and take a break.    I'm sure I'll take up the next one when it's written. Here's hoping it's less indulgent and more focused than \\"The Wise Man's Fear.\\"

B004G600A4|4.0|The 50th animated feature in the Walt Disney Animated Classics series, \\"Tangled\\" is a little less flashy and a little less hectic than the average animated feature these days, hewing closer to \\"reality\\" than most such films - if one can stretch that term to include a tale of a beautiful, tower-dwelling princess whose blonde tresses glow when she sings, and whose \\"Prince Charming\\" comes in the form of a roguish thief whose face is plastered on wanted posters all over the countryside.  But \\"Tangled\\" doesn't overplay its hand in the storytelling arena.  There are no fire-breathing dragons, no talking animals, no over-extended action sequences (or at least no more than necessary).  The focus is mainly on two attractive and likable characters coming to know and accept one another throughout the course of their adventures.<br /><br />The adventure begins when Rapunzel (Mandy Moore), who has just turned eighteen, decides she wants to leave the castle in which she's been locked away by an old crone posing as her mother - it turns out Rapunzel's hair confers eternal youth on anyone who touches it - in order to experience what life is like in the outside world.  Rapunzel finally gets her wish when the thief, Flynn Rider (Zachary Levi) - who's fleeing the law and the two cohorts he's just betrayed - attempts to hide in her tower, which he assumes is empty.  However, he gets more than he bargained for when he meets this na챦ve but feisty young woman who sees him as the opportunity for escape she's been pining for all these years.  So off they go to see the world, with many an unexpected adventure awaiting them along the way.<br /><br />\\"Tangled\\" is a little more deliberately paced than the typical kids' flick - indeed it takes a full half-hour before we even get outside the tower room - and the scenes run a little longer than usual, as Rapunzel and Flynn take the time to establish a relationship of mutual respect and trust.  The humor is gentle and the tone sweet, yet there is just the right amount of starch in the characters of the crone (nicely voiced by Donna Murphy) and Flynn's aggrieved criminal cohorts to keep things from getting too sappy.  The soft-focus backgrounds provide an appropriately comfy setting for the tale, and the musical numbers (by Alan Menken and Glenn Slater) have an appealing Broadway show-tune brassiness to them that lifts the audience's spirits.<br /><br />As written by Dan Fogelman and directed by Nathan Greno and Byron Howard, \\"Tangled\\" doesn't come sailing out of the wings, as so many similar recent films have done, trying to impress us with its postmodern revisionism and cynical self-awareness.  It tweaks some of the elements of the classic Fairy Tale genre to be sure, but it also acknowledges and respects those elements.  It really isn't in the business of tearing things down just so it can congratulate itself on its own iconoclasm and irreverence.  And I, for one, am tremendously grateful for that.amount of starch in the characters of the crone (nicely voiced by Donna Murphy) and Flynn's aggrieved criminal cohorts to keep things from getting too sappy. I remember playing violin for three hours a day (I was great, and wouldn't have been without that push), I remember my dad ordering me collectible stamps one-sheets at the age of 8(and then having me write a two-page paper on the information, doing more research if I didn't have enough content.  I'm a great writer now), I remember doing the math workbooks you could find at the bookstore and working two grades ahead (I am miserable at Math.  So they dropped the ball on that one) and I remember being held to the highest standards of courtesy, respect and dignity when in public and with relatives (but really, shouldn't all kids?).    I remember being pushed to always do better and I wouldn't be successful as I would be now if my parents hadn't.  Honestly, the Chinese do hold their children to higher standards, and those Asians out there that disagree had weird parents.  I didn't mind being raised \\"in the Chinese way\\" at all.  Mind you, it was a bit more subtle than the way that Amy Chua recounts, but it was definitely the Chinese way.  I was allowed to pick my passions and pursuits. But my parents made sure as heck that if they were paying for my pursuits, that I'd better excel at them, above and beyond the rest of the pack.    Brava, Amy Chua.  Thank you for a wonderful, humorous, accurate and nostalgic trip back into my childhood.  You did a great job.

However, many of these bon mots come from the observations of Lord Harry, who is the cynic that leads Dorian Gray down the road of temptation and vice. However, he begins the book as An Ideal - the physical embodiment of all that is good, youthful, innocent and free of sin. In fact, his ideal purity is what inspires his friend, Basil, to paint the titular picture of Dorian Gray. Once Dorian makes the acquaintance of Basil's friend, Lord Harry, however, Dorian is fascinated by Lord Harry's worldly and cosmopolitan views. In a burst of youthful enthusiasm, Dorian wishes for the picture to bear the physical outcomes of his actions so that Dorian can retain his beauty. He soon discovers that his wish has been granted. Adopting Lord Harry's way of life, Dorian descends into a lifestyle of vice and pleasure seeking and perversely (and childishly?) anticipates the devastation he can wreak on the portrait without bearing any evidence on his own physical form.<br /><br />The levels of Harry's influence, Dorian's self-destruction, and Dorian's blindness to the true elements of his fall from \\"goodness\\" combined to make for a powerful read. All are to some degree aware of Lincoln's demise.  Few would have any knowledge of the broader conspiracy.<br /><br />Robert Redford is to be commended for bringing this story to a wider audience. In this case, it is the use of almost summary justice whereby individuals can be tried before a military commission rather than a jury of their peers. She was never to be allowed a fair trial.<br /><br />The obvious analogy being worked here is the current events in Guantanomo Bay. It was played almost entirely straight like a classic western (a la [[ASIN:B00006JMRE High Noon (Collector's Edition)]], [[ASIN:B000059TFW The Magnificent Seven (Special Edition)]], and [[ASIN:0792163710 Shane]]) with just enough humor to break the tension in order to rekindle it to new heights later in the film.  Shakespeare would have been proud--Heck, Shakespeare would have had fun with the concept had it occurred to him!<br /><br />The story partakes of a bit of both sci-fi and the western, creating a sort of \\"what if?\\" ambiance.  It pits the grit of late 19th century Southwesterners intent on reclaiming their family memebers, dead or alive, or die trying against a higher tech race of aliens bent on taking over the planet--or at least its gold supply.  Though the weapons of the former are primitive by comparison to their enemies, their ability to bury their differences and risk their lives for a common goal is their strong suite.  Furthermore, it is ultimately not their vulnerability that counts but that of their alien enemies.  The aliens are at base no more immortal or immune to harm than humans are, and their apparent contempt for humans proves their undoing.<br /><br />The use of CGI in the film was admirably restrained.  In short it was never introduced to the point where it dominated the screen or the live actors on it.  The animated characters were there as the \\"bad guys,\\" not to impress us with the quality of computer graphics---with which even the elderly are probably more than familiar by now.<br /><br />The primary heroes were all consummate actors; Harrison Ford, who is very used to the concept of animation and blue screen techniques, seemed especially comfortable in his role, and Danial Craig was wonderful.  His deadpan affect was just what was needed in the role of anti-hero pressed into the service of mankind by circumstances.  I also appreciated the director's take on a well shaped derri&egrave;re framed by leather chaps!  The heroine, Olivia Wilde, was mysterious and engaging; her placement in a role of self sacrifice was moving; so often it is a male character that makes the ultimate sacrifice for human kind, making it seem as though women are either unwilling or incapable of doing so.  It was a good choice.  So was the fact that she did not return to give the film a standard \\"happy ending.\\"  Again a remarkable sense of restraint in a film that could have gone way over the top.<br /><br />In all an entertaining film.l Craig was wonderful. 0452286751|5.0|It's hard to know what else to say about a book like this because The Fountainhead has been praised, analyzed, discussed and criticized from all points of view by so many people.<br /><br />I will simply say that I think this book is terrific on every level.  The characters are unique and interesting and what they stand for ties them into Rand's personal philosophy of objectivism.  And although I think Rand's beliefs are extreme, I admire Roark's unwillingness to compromise his designs.  Rand's ability to develop these characters, weave them into a complex and interesting story and keep the reader going through all 700+ pages is a genius accomplishment that stands the test of time.<br /><br />My favorite part of the book is the friendship that develops between Roark and Wynand because they both let down their guard and become human beings.  If you have to read this for school, be patient.  It is worth the effort and it will keep you thinking!
B004Y1USV2|4.0|I've been listening to Fly From Here for more than a month now and so far I'm still soaring from it. Got mine earlier thru a friend from Tokyo and the best part of having Japanese cd imports, you get bonus tracks. In this case, the full version of \\"Hour Of Need\\"- this track rocks from start to end. They should have released this version here in the states so as to prove to the naysayers that they're absolutely wrong in all aspects of this rejuvenated band. Pulled out my Yes catalog (feeling a bit nostalgic there) from day one and even the Buggles' Adventures In Modern Recording just for comparison's sake. Yep! It's there but this new release is not even close to being a Buggles' album. Video Killed The Radio Star? Wow, the intrigue! The comparison is pathetically lame. Drama? Maybe so but Drama had the raw heaviness of the eighties when disco, punk and new wave were at their peak. In my opinion, Fly From Here is definitely your Yes album - still progressive but melodious with less complexity yet classic. I love calling it progressive AOR. At this day and age, I don't expect any hits coming out of it. It's Lady Gaga and Black Eyed Peas all over the airwaves! We should all smell the coffee! This is Yes music for Yes lovers. Not the kind you'd be flaunting on the streets unless you want people staring at you. I really don't have to go into details of it's content and essence since most of you heard it and said your piece. Whether good or bad, it's your matter of opinion. For the past four decades, this band has been a progenitor of classic progressive rock and has spawned other bands of the same genre. Yes is a very unique band. The lineup kept on changing and still is. Where can you find a band that had more musical talents than anywhere else of this pedigree? Keyboardists, drummers, guitarists, even lead singers. Chris Squire is thus the last man standing! Well, of course, besides the Anderson, Bruford, Wakeman and Howe album which he's not involved in but Tony Levin (King Crimson, Peter Gabriel), still I consider it a Yes album. Yes is all about the music, not personalities. I love Jon for all he is but Yes is Yes, not Jon is Yes. The whole is greater than the sum of it's parts - think synergy. To each his own. Simply stated, I DON'T CARE who's who in it as long as the music gets my old and rusty prog engines running, no problem at all. As far as I'm concerned, Yes is a faceless entity - it does not identify itself with its members. If ever this band is to bring down the curtains due to the complexities of life and age, at least it ended with Fly From Here and perhaps time will be kinder to it for it to be their swan song and be part of the Yes legacy but even then, I still look forward to more great Yes music after Fly From Here. It is unfortunate for the overzealous purist who refuses this change to learn that this current release has the popular Yes logo printed and painted all over it with two coats even. BTW, love the Roger Dean artwork on Fly From Here - a very nice continuum for the box set, In A Word Yes (1969 - and add 2011 in it).(King Crimson, Peter Gabriel), still I consider it a Yes album. B0054R6BFM|3.0|Overall: 3 1/2 stars<br /><br />Plot/Storyline:  3 3/4 stars<br /><br />It's a great idea, right? So cool of a concept that I read the article and purchased the book as soon as I could.  I love the concept, but am a little less enamored when it comes to the execution of the story and to matters of characterization.<br /><br />As far as it goes, I think it's a helpful book for teens. Any story that preaches that bullying and casual cruelties have consequences is a needed message. The issue becomes what responsibility Hannah has for her choice and if the punishment fits the crimes.  If nothing else, the discussions it opens up will be interesting.<br /><br />Hannah says to these people that what they did lead to what she did and, by implication, they should carry the guilt of that. I suspect most readers will think that in the case of one or two of the people they should have to live with and struggle with that. However, some of the people were rude, or ignorant, or mean, or even casually cruel, but it's arguable if it's fair to expect them to carry the burden of Hannah's decision - particularly in the case of a girl who was a victim herself or a guidance counselor whom she only met one time and set up for failure.<br /><br />While the premise is good, I couldn't help but think that it needed to take two paths -- one of benevolence and education toward people who could not have known or one of anger toward people who were truly reprehensible. The difficulty is her anger toward both groups as if their \\"sins\\" were equal. Ultimately, this choice will put some readers in the uncomfortable position of realizing they dislike, or disapprove of, a girl who took her own life.<br /><br />There was also the small detail that she threatened that if people didn't listen and pass it on that another party would make sure that the tapes got out - but I can't imagine that the adult recipient wouldn't contact the authorities based on details in the tape. I also can't imagine Hannah not knowing that.<br /><br />Lastly, the reason why Clay was on the list seemed too easy, a bit off a cop-out, even a cheat, since - without saying more - he stood alone.  This removed some of the power I might have felt.<br /><br />Characters: 3 1/4 stars<br /><br />There are two main characters here. Hannah and Clay. We meet the other characters who have received or will receive the take, sometimes during the course of Clay's travels around town as he listens, but mostly through Hannah's narration. As mentioned, when she speaks we also read Clay's reactions to her words. Clay is pretty likable character. Some of the other characters are villainous. Others seem like kids making stupid mistakes.<br /><br />I believe that we were supposed to see Clay as someone who could have saved her, if only he was more confident, if only he had listened to rumors less. For once, I don't think Hannah put the burden on someone else so much as Clay felt understandable guilt at not reaching out to her sooner. (Understandable in the sense of it being a natural reaction, not in the sense of being justified.)<br /><br />As mentioned, I had complex feelings toward Hannah. Obviously there's built in sympathy for her, but it was hard to read her deliberate cruelty toward others. Suicide can sometimes be a very angry act, a way to punish others for real or perceived grievances, and the choice can be made for what seems to the outside world to be petty, but feels like the weight of the world to the person in question. Understanding all of this doesn't change the fact that Hannah comes across as vindictive and looking for scapegoats.<br /><br />I don't get the feeling I was supposed to agree with Hannah completely, but I do get the impression I wasn't supposed to be as irritated with her as much as I was, which was about 60% of the time.  While teens are self-involved and this one was in a bad place, she was surprisingly difficult to root for in her efforts and I - yeah, I'm going to hell - never felt particularly saddened that the girl speaking had shuffled off this mortal coil.  The inherent poignancy of the story she was telling couldn't always rise above what seemed to be a need to punish. Think of Carrie, without the supernatural stuff, with 100% less pig's blood, and with more time on her hands and a tape recorder.<br /><br />On the plus side, there were a fair amount of minor characters, and the author did a pretty good job with them. I felt like I knew them.<br /><br />Writing Style:  3 3/4 stars<br /><br />Several nice moments, but in many ways this felt like a first book.  This book called for a balance between Hannah's telling her story and Clay reacting and also moving around town as he listened, and it never felt like the author quite worked out the right ratio. Many of the descriptions were good, places were described vividly in several cases, but at other moments it all felt a little flat.erent poignancy of the story she was telling couldn't always rise above what seemed to be a need to punish. Think of Carrie, without the supernatural stuff, with 100% less pig's blood, and with more time on her hands and a tape recorder.    On the plus side, there were a fair amount of minor characters, and the author did a pretty good job with them. I felt like I knew them. 0060558121|1.0|I understand Gaiman's establishment in the genre and his accolades. Considerably, American Gods has won the Hugo award (one of the most prestigious sci-fi awards). However, as a novel of fiction this book is a mediocre attempt at what really is a post-modern thematic inquiry into mythology and what that means. The narrative pacing is slow (nothing wrong with this) but it's problematic because it seems aimless for most of the book. Yes, the point is for the main character to introduce us to the various deities of world mythology in all of their regression and decrepitness. To me, it seems the purpose of this novel functions as a look at gods and how they've changed from the old establishments to the 'new' gods (technology, food, etc...) and their mythical impending battle. We are given small clever glimpses into who is where and their ambiguous purposes in America too. But all of this seems to fall short of what should really be happening: the art of telling the story. Although, Gaiman pulls some of this together in the end, one can't help wondering what the point of it all is except being a commentary on human nature and our society's beliefs. Rather, this would be a better essay in mythology and post-modernism mixed with philosophy of human belief structures because it doesn't work so well in this narrative.<br /><br />Some might be disappointed about how the deities are portrayed; degenerate, lecherous, and small. Mythology has a tendency to provide many with grand illusions of might and power. Gaiman makes a real statement here about this and my criticism doesn't fault him for this perspective. Overall, this novel really just lacks a compelling story and places an ambiguous importance upon once thriving gods that function aimlessly in the background of the story. I'd suggest reading some of the preview pages that Amazon provides and it may give you a glimpse of what is there.

B000068QZK|5.0|Ozzy Osbourne's No Rest For The Wicked was his fifth studio album as a solo artist and was released in 1988. This was the first Ozzy album to feature Zack Wlyde who replaced Jake E Lee on guitar duties.<br /><br />Zack has a very different guitar style to either Randy's or Jake E Lee's; his playing is full of pinch harmonics, squeals and other little flourishes that make his playing different to each of Ozzy's previous guitarists, on top of that he delivers a more heavy metal style of riff more frequently and as a consequence the direction of this album changed.<br /><br />After the death of original guitarist Randy Rhodes, Ozzy started heading in a more commercial direction, but No Rest For The Wicked reverse the trend with a very rock orientated sound musically and much heavier production sound.<br /><br />No Rest For The Wicked primarily contains straight up hard and heavy numbers, with a much less commercial direction than the previous two albums and as such hasn't dated just as badly as they have. Surprisingly; give the increased heaviness of the album, it sold very well and was a large commercial and critical success.<br /><br />The album is full of strong and well written music, including the excellent `Miracle Man,' `Breaking All The Rules,' and `Crazy Babies.' The whole album is fairly consistent and the majority of the material is of that same high quality.<br /><br />If you enjoy big riffs and bold guitar solos then this is a superb album, the only real flaw you could level at it is that it may be a bit repetitive, certainly so if you don't like pinch harmonics. For everyone else, this shouldn't put you off however and I highly recommend you give the album a shot.<br /><br />Overall, No Rest For The Wicked is one of the better Ozzy albums out there, the material is strong, consistent and fairly heavy. Overall, it is a pretty essential buy for fans.

B004YTGRMI|4.0|Everyone else has debated the merits of this album here so I'm not going to go into a lot of detail about the artistic quality of the album except to say that this remains one of my favorite REM albums. \\"Lifes Rich Pageant\\" saw the formula of the first three albums altered alteration of the band's once murky sound in favor of clear, clean vocals and instrumentation. Producer Don Gehman ushered the band more into the mainstream with this album helping the band fashion a strong ROCK record that focused less on mysterious sonics and more on straight ahead Rock 'n' Roll.<br /><br />Interestingly, Geham (according to the liner notes) convinced Stipe to deliver lyrics with a message that fans could understand but if you listen to the demos it seems he was already headed in that direction with much clearer diction so, perhaps, the band was already headed down the less rocky path of the indie band it was and decided to swerve into the lane reserved for mainstream rock artists.<br /><br />The remaster is another matter entirely. While the detail is extremely good here on the remaster, it is compressed and loud sounding--there's no subtly (I'm not sure if there should be any but I doubt the mastertape sounds like this)to the presentation or the soundtstage on this remastered 25th Anniversary Edition. It suffers from many of the sins that have dogged other remasters including REM's recent deluxe reissues. My advice--hold on to whatever version of this sounds best to you because I doubt sincerely you're going to love this unless you're listening on earbuds from an iPod or mp3 player (or small computer speakers). You can then decide which one you like best.  Clearly that's what this remaster was designed for.  I suspect the decision to go with the compressed version here came from REM themselves. That said, the EQ choices for the mastering aren't quite as harsh as many remasters and there is a bit of dynamic range although peak limited appears to be used.<br /><br />The real reason most fans are going to buy this set isn't because of the deluxe packaging--the booklet which is a measeley 12 pages doesn't provide quite the immersively experience for most boxed sets. The postcards are nice although hardly essential and the same can be said for the poster (most fans are of an age where a poster really wouldn't be high on their reasons to buy this)--it's because of the disc of demos. I do wish that Capitol/IRS/EMI had gone for a THREE disc set with a live disc from one of their better shows on the tour here in addition to the original album AND the demos disc.<br /><br />The demos disc features 19 songs including some material that didn't make the final album (and some tracks that have never appeared in finished form anywhere). The material that appears here but didn't make the final album includes \\"Two Steps Onward\\", \\"Out of Tune\\", \\"Wait\\", \\"All The Right Friends\\" and \\"March Song\\" (which became \\"King of Birds\\"). There are also VERY rough drafts of some songs including \\"I Believe\\" where Michael Stipe just hums the melody of the unfinished song that are fascinating as it's like listening to the creation of the songs themselves and being allowed to hear avenues the band elected NOT to turn down for certain songs.<br /><br />My only major issue is with the mastering of the album (and the demos disc); people who are going to buy the CD are buying it NOT because they want to hear it louder but because they want to hear the best possible sounding version of the album on CD possible. Those who want the compressed, louder version will, no doubt, download it.<br /><br />I am disappointed that we keep going in this direction with remasters. If you want to hear this album the way it SHOULD have sounded my advice is to not purchase the CD but buy the vinyl (if you have a turn table)as Mobile Fidelity mastered the vinyl version (just go to their website and see about ordering it)and that, at least, promises a good sounding version of this album.<br /><br />Recommended but only for the second disc of demos NOT the mastering of the main album.<br /><br />5 stars for the album itself (and the second disc of demos)<br />3 stars for the masteringhe mastering of the main album.    5 stars for the album itself (and the second disc of demos)  3 stars for the mastering

0375836675|4.0|The Messenger is Markus Zusak's 4th novel. The story revolves around Ed Kennedy, an under-age taxi driver in a small town somewhere in rural Australia. Ed's life seems pretty ordinary: he lives in a suburban shack, shares coffee with his smelly dog, The Doorman, and plays cards with friends Marv, Ritchie and Audrey (with whom he's in love). Things start to change when he inadvertently stops a bank robbery: the first ace, the ace of diamonds turns up in his letterbox, and it appears he has been chosen to affect the lives of certain others. But who is sending the aces? Who has chosen him to be the messenger?   Zusak captures the feel of small town Australia very well, probably from his own experience. The story is a bit slow-moving in parts (just like small-town life). Zusak gives us characters to like, love and hate, dialogue that is authentic, some humour and an intriguing plot. Without giving anything away, some readers may be disappointed in the ending, but the last few pages bear rereading and contemplation. At first I felt a bit cheated, like I did after reading Ian McEwan's Atonement, but I found it easier to read than that novel and, on reflection, I liked the ending. It is certainly not a children's book, even though it was the winner of the 2003 Children's Book Council Book of the Year Award!
